This is a re-published version of the attached final specification. 
For this re-published version, the prior versions of the IPR Policy will apply, except that the previous requirement 
for Adopters (as defined in the earlier IPR Policy) to agree to an O-RAN Adopter License Agreement to access 
and use Final Specifications shall no longer apply or be required for these Final Specifications after 1st July 2022.
The copying or incorporation into any other work of part or all of the material available in this specification in 
any form without the prior written permission of O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.  is prohibited, save that you may print 
or download extracts of the material on this site for your personal use, or copy the material on this site for the 
purpose of sending to individual third parties for their information provided that you acknowledge O-RAN 
ALLIANCE as the source of the material and that you inform the third party that these conditions apply to them 
and that they must comply with them.

following formal O-RAN approval. Should the O-RAN.org modify the contents of the present document, it will be 5 
re-released by O -RAN Alliance with an identifying change of release date and an increase in version number as  6 
follows: 7 
Release x.y.z 8 
where: 9 
x the first digit is incremented for all changes of substance, i.e. technical enhancements, 10 
corrections, updates, etc. (the initial approved document will have x=01). 11 
y the second digit is incremented when editorial only chan ges have been incorporated in 12 
the document. 13 
z the third digit included only in working versions of the document indicating 14 
incremental changes during the editing process. This variable is for internal WG7 use 15 
only. 16

1.3 Definitions and Abbreviations 2 
1.3.1 Definitions 3 
For the purposes of the present document, the terms and definitions given in 3GPP TR  21.905 [1] and the 4 
following apply. A term defined in the present document takes precedence over the definition of the same term, if 5 
any, in 3GPP TR  21.905 [2]. For the base station classes of Pico, Micro and Macro, the definitions are given in 6 
3GPP TR 38.104 [3]. 7 
Carrier Frequency:  Center frequency of the cell.  8 
F1 interface:  The open interface between O-CU and O-DU8 as defined by 3GPP TS 38.473 between CU and DU. 9 
Integrated architecture:  In the integrated architecture, the O -RU and O -DUx are implemented on one platform. 10 
Each O-RU and RF front  end is associated with one  O-DU8. They are then aggregated to O -CU and connected by 11 
F1 interface. 12 
Split architecture:  The O -RUx and O-DUx are physically separated from one another in this architecture. A 13 
switch may aggregate multiple O -RUxs to one O -DUx.  O-DUx switch and O-RUxs are connected by the fronthaul 14 
interface as defined in WG4. 15 
Transmission Reception Point (TRxP ):  Antenna array with one or more antenna elements available to the 16 
network located at a specific geographical location for a specific area. 17

Chapter 2 Hardware Reference Design 1 1 
This chapter describes a white box hardware reference design example for indoor Picocell deployment scenario.  It 2 
includes O-CU, O-DU8 O-RU8, and FHGW8 for IPC deployment scenario. 3 
2.1 O-CU Hardware Reference Design 4 
The O-CU white box hardware is the platform that perform the O-CU function of upper l2 and l3. The hardware 5 
systems specified in this document meet the computing, power and environmental requirements of use cases 6 
configurations and feature sets of RAN physical node. These requirements are described in the hardware 7 
architecture and requirement specification [6] as well as in the use cases document [1]. The O-CU hardware 8 
includes the chassis platform, mother board, peripheral devices and cooling devices. The mother board contains 9 
processing unit, memory, the internal I/O interfaces, and external connection ports. The midhaul (MH) and 10 
backhaul (BH) interface are used to carry the traffic between O-CU and O-DU8 as well as O-CU and core network. 11 
The other hardware functional components include: the storage for software, hardware and system debugging 12 
interfaces, board management controller, just to name a few; the O-CU designer will make decision based on the 13 
specific needs of the implementation. 14 
The HW reference design of O-CU is the same as o-du8 except for the need of HW accelerator, thus detail design 15 
will be described in o-du8 section 2.2. 16 
2.2 O-DU8 Hardware Reference Design  17 
The O-DU8 white box hardware  is the platform that performs the DU function of L1 and lower L2. The hardware 18 
systems specified in this document meet the computing , power and environmental requirements of use cases 19 
configurations and feature sets of RAN physical node. These requirements are descr ibed in the early hardware 20 
requirement specification as well as in the use cases document. The O -DU8 hardware includes the chassis platform, 21 
mother board, peripheral devices and cooling devices. The mother board contains processing unit, memory, the 22 
internal I/O interfaces, and external connection ports. The fronthaul and backhaul interface are used to carry the 23 
traffic between O -RU8/FHGW8 and O-DU8 as well as O -CU and O -DU8. The O -DU8 design may also provide an 24 
interface for hardware accelerator if that op tion is preferred. The other hardware functional components include: 25 
the storage for software, hardware and system debuggi ng interfaces, board management controller, just to name a 26 
few; the O-DU8 designer will make decision based on the specific needs of the implementation. 27 
Note that the O-DU8 HW reference design is also feasible for O-CU and integrated O-CU/ O-DU8. 28 
2.2.1 O-DU8 High-Level Functional Block Diagram 29 
Figure 2 -1 shows the major functional blocks of O -DU8. The d igital processing unit handles the baseband 30 
processing workload. To make the processing more efficient, an accelerator can be used to assist wi th the baseband 31 
workload processing. The memory devices include the random -access memory (RAM) for temporary stor age of 32 
data while flash memory is used for codes and logs. The storage device is for persistent storage. The external 33

network cards can be used for fronthaul or backhaul connection. The baseboard management controller (BMC) is a 1 
microcontroller which monitors hardware operation on motherboard. The clock circuits provide digital processing 2 
unit with required clock signals. 3

As described in the previous section, the O-DU8 hardware can be implemented with difference design choices. 1 
Here, a system-on-a-chip (SoC) based design is presented as an example which processes most of the O-DU8 2 
workload.  The accelerator can be used to perform some O-DU8 workload functions based on the overall 3 
performance requirement. Several Ethernet controllers are used for front haul link, back haul link and remote 4 
console control connection. The other parts include: RAM, flash memory, and hard drive storage. The JTAG and  5 
USB ports are provided for hardware debug and local connection if needed. Figure 2-2 describes the components 6 
and connections of O-DU8 hardware.  Finally, BMC block is mainly responsible for monitoring the hardware status. 7 
2.2.3 O-DU8 Hardware Components 8 
In this section, t he details of the O -DU8 hardware component’s requirements, features and parameters are 9 
described. The components selection is based on the use case requireme nts which are listed in the hardware 10 
architecture and requirements document [6]. 11

PCIe:  PCIe Gen 3 should be supported by the processor. There are total of 32 PCIe lanes with 128 Gb/s 1 
bandwidth. The 32 PCIe lanes can be divided into two x16 slots by using a riser card.  2 
Ethernet:  The system should be capable to offer aggregated 48 Gb/ s Ethernet bandwidth. The breakout 3 
the ports are discussed in later section. When higher Ethernet bandwidth required, an Ethernet card can be 4 
installed in one of the PCIe slot.  5 
b. Digital Processing Unit Design 6 
The digital processing unit is a SoC device which is a  64-bit multi-core server class processor. This SoC 7 
includes an integrated Platform Controller Hub (PCH), integrated high -speed I/O, Integrated Memory 8 
Controllers (IMC), and four integrated 10 Gigabit Ethernet ports. 9 
The SOC supports 512- bit wide vector pr ocessing instruction set. It also supports hardware virtualization 10 
to enable dynamic provisioning of services as communication service providers extend network functions 11 
virtualization (NFV). Figure 2-2 shows the major functional blocks of the digital processing unit. 12 
2.2.3.2 Hardware Accelerator 13 
Hardware accelerators can be used in O -DU8 to offload computationally intensive functions and to optimize the 14 
performance under varying traffic and loading conditions. The acceleration functional requ irements and 15 
implementation are system designer’s choice; however, the O -DU8 shall meet the minimu m system performance 16 
requirements under various loading and deployment conditions. In most cases, a Field Programmable Gate Array  17 
(FPGA) or Application Specif ic Integrated Circuit (ASIC) based PCIe card can be used to optimize the system 18 
performance. The F PGA(s) are part of a Network Interface Controller (NIC) that further provides connectivity 19 
services. 20 
2.2.3.2.1 Accelerator Design Solution 1 21 
The O-DU8 system is typical ly implemented using a multi -core processor and one or more hardware accelerators. 22 
Parts of O -DU8 protocol stack can be implemented in software running on the multi -core processors, some of the 23 
computationally intensive L1 and L2 functions are offloaded to  FPGA-based or similar hardware accelerators. This 24 
is a programmable hardware, which provides both flexibility and high computing capabilities.  25 
a. Accelerator Requirements 26 
The accelerator unit comprises one or more FPGAs (e.g., two FPGAs), sufficient amount of DDR4 27 
memory, and synchronization circuitry where one of FPGAs is used for L1 functional offloa d and the 28 
other one is used to perform fronthaul connectivity functions/protocols. The FPGA for L1 offload uses 29 
dedicated cores for channel encoding/decoding as well as FPGA and processing resources for running L1 30 
functions such as but not limited to rate matching and de -matching, interleaving and scrambling, 31 
demodulation and HARQ buffer management as well as OFDM modulation/demodulation and channel 32 
estimation.  33 
Key features of the FPGA-based accelerator include: 34 
 2X10/25G CPRI or RoE fronthaul interface 35 
 Built-in SyncE/IEEE1588v2 synchronization + external reference timing 36 
 L1 offloading options 37 
- LDPC encoding and decoding  38 
- Polar encoding and decoding  39 
- HARQ management with on board DDR memory (including DDR controller and interfaces)  40 
- Other L1 offloading candida tes include PRACH detection, MIMO encoding and decoding, 41 
channel estimation 42 

 O-RAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
Copyright 2020 O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.                     
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in the Annex ZZZ  22 
 
- Partial or full L1 functions can be offloaded. It is recommended to offload the use r-plane 1 
channel coding chain and part of or the entire control -plane channel coding chain to the 2 
hardware accelerator. 3 
 PCIe Gen3 x16, two Gen4 x8, or PCIe Gen3 x16 bifurcated to two Gen3 x8 4 
 GPP supported 5 
 Standard PCIe FHHL card (It is assumed that the hard ware accelerators further perform NIC 6 
functions). 7 
 8GB DDR4 memory for buffering 8 
 Power consumption not to exceed 75W 9

 1 
The following are the accelerator requirements in function and interface. 2 
Functional offload requirements 3 
One of the candidate functions for offloading is the LDPC encoder and decoder, which typically consists 4 
of computationally intensive and relatively highly power -consuming functions. It must be noted that 5 
neither software implementation in CPU nor soft FPGA logic implementation would pro vide a highly 6 
power-efficient solution while meeting/exceeding 3GPP NR user -plane encoder/decoder throughput and 7 
latency requirements, rather a hardened implementation of the FEC functions would be very power 8 
efficient. Downlink and uplink throughputs of up to 40Gbps and 18Gbps, respectively, are shown feasible 9 
with this architect ure. Other candidate L1 functions for acceleration include CRC generation, 10 
segmentation, bit-level/sub-block interleaving and scrambling as well as FFT/IFFT processing, for which 11 
an FPGA can be used. 12 
For other symbol processing L1 functions, which require heavy multiply and accumulation operations, 13 
FPGAs 1 and 2 have DSP blocks that can efficiently perform these operations. Polar encoding and/or 14 
decoding on the control -plane can als o be offloaded to FPGA 1 resulting in high throughputs and low 15 
latencies. 16 
Interface requirements 17 
- PCIe: PCIe interface is widely used to provide interface between the GPP and hardware accelerators. 18 
FPGA devices have dedicated PCIe hard IP which facilitate implementation and quick setup of PCIe 19 
interface. They support both PCIe Gen 3 x16 or PCIe Gen4 x8, which allow the FPGA device to interface 20 
with any GPP supporting either PCIe Gen3 x16 or PCIe Gen4 x8 interface. 21 
- Fronthaul: FPGA devices can support variou s speed grades and any fronthaul protocols, customers can 22 
use off-the-shelf CPRI or RoE IPs to quickly implement and configure any fronthaul interface protocol. 23 
- Serial transceivers:  FPGA devices have SerDes resources to implement various connectivity speeds 24 
(e.g., 33 Gb/s) per SerDes, 10G/25G CPRI, 10G/25G/50G/100G Ethernet conn ections can use these 25 
SerDes resources. 26 
- Ethernet MAC speed:  FPGA devices have hardened implementations of Ethernet MAC that support 27 
speeds of 100 Gb/s and above. The Ethernet MAC  IP allows power -efficient implementation of high 28 
speed Ethernet connectivit y. In the example shown in Figure 2-3, FPGA 1 and FPGA 2 can use the hard 29 
100Gbps MAC IPs to connect each other, allowing the L1 and fronthaul functions to be distributed across 30 
these two FPGAs with less connectivity overhead. For  other Ethernet MACs such as 10G/25G Ethernet, 31 

 O-RAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
Copyright 2020 O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.                     
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in the Annex ZZZ  24 
 
they provide soft Ethernet MAC IPs, so when implementing CPRI or RoE fronthaul functions, 10G/25G 1 
Ethernet MAC can be used. 2

depicts multi -lane Gen3 or Gen4 PCIe interfaces between each FPGA and the CPU. The FPGAs 7 
communicate through high-bandwidth Ethernet (GbE) transport. 8

a. Accelerator Requirement 9 
The accelerator unit comprises one or more FPGAs (e.g ., two FPGAs), sufficient amount of DDR4 10 
memory, and synchronization circuitry where one of FPGAs is used for L1 functional offload and the 11 
other one is used to perform fronthaul connectivity functions/p rotocols. The FPGA for L1 offload uses 12 
dedicated cores for channel encoding/decoding as well as FPGA and processing resources for running L1 13 
functions such as but not limited to rate matching and de -matching, interleaving and scrambling, 14 
demodulation and H ARQ buffer management as well as OFDM modulation/demo dulation and channel 15 
estimation.  16 
Key features of the FPGA-based accelerator include: 17 
 4X25G/10G CPRI optical interface 18 
 1x10G Ethernet optical interface 19 
 Support GNSS/IEEE1588v2+syncE  reference timing 20 
 L1 offloading options 21 
- LDPC encoding and decoding  22

- HARQ management 1 
- Other optional L1 offloading such as FFT/IFFT, CP, CRC, rate matching, PRACH detection.  2 
 PCIe Gen3X16, 4 hard IP blocks 3 
 FHHL form factor and operation temperature from -5℃ to +55℃ 4 
 2GB DDR4 memory for Buffering 5 
 Specific power supply design is requ ired with 1 -chip FPGA, since the maximum power 6 
consumption of PCIe specification (75w) could be exceeded, additional external auxiliary power 7 
may be required. 8 
Functional offload requirements 9 
- L1 offloading: One of the functions for L1 offloading is the LD PC encoder and decoder, which 10 
typically consists of computationally -intensive and relatively highly power -consuming functions. Other 11 
L1 functions for acceleration include CRC generation, CP, PR ACH detection and rate matching as well as 12 
FFT/IFFT processing. 13 
- Fronthaul: The data transmission and protocol processing of fronthaul interface is completed on the 14 
FPGA device. 15 
Interface requirements 16 
- PCIe: PCIe interface is widely used to provide inter face between the CPU and hardware accelerators. 17 
FPGA device has  dedicated PCIe hard IP which facilitate implementation and quick setup of PCIe 18 
interface. FPGA support PCIe Gen3 x16, which allow the FPGA device to interface with any CPU 19 
supporting either PCIe Gen3 x16 interface. 20 
- Fronthaul: FPGA device can support var ious speed grades and CPRI protocol for fronthaul interface 21 
between O-DU8 and O-RU8.  22 
- Backhaul: FPGA device can support backhaul interface between O-DU8 and core network function with 23 
an 10G optical interface. 24 
- Synchronization: Accelerator has external interfaces to support synchronization, including GNSS, 25 
IEEE1588v2 and SyncE reference timing.  26

Figure 2-5 shows the accelerator design, description of block diagram of board card: 1 
12V power: The maximum power consumption of the whole card is estimated to exceed 75W. The PCIe 2 
cannot meet the power consumption demand, so it needs an e xternal 12V auxiliary power supply. The 3 
extra power consumption can be included by the power unit of O -DU8. Only power cord is needed, and it 4 
has no effect on the whole machine design. This part is not necessary if there are future optimizations. 5 
DC/DC: The module contains all the power sources used in the system. 6 
Power Supply Sequencer and Monitor: Since the system has the power on timing requirement, this 7 
module controls the output of each DC/DC to realize the normal power on/off of the system. 8 
Mixer and GNSS antenna: The system uses an external antenna, which can be placed outdoors to 9 
improve the receiving sensitivity of synchronizing signal. After mixer’s processing of the received signal, 10 
the signal source of 1pps is output to clock chip for system input synchronous clock signal.  11 
MCU (micro control unit):  As the auxiliary processor of the board card, MCU realizes the temperature 12 
detection of the board card, the operation state indication and the c onfiguration of power monitor and 13 
clock chip in the initialization process, and real -time monitoring of the working state of each module, 14 
exception handling and other functions. 15 
Clock chip: The chip realizes system -level clock input, which can synchronousl y output all kinds of 16 
clocks for clock synchronization of subordinate modules. 17 
FPGA: As the core chip of the board card, FPGA forwards the data of the downlink of the core network 18 
and the uplink data gathered at the O -RU8(s), and interacts with the O -DU8 server through PCIe x16 to 19 
complete the calculation of 5G NR data. 20 
2.2.3.2.3 Accelerator Design Solution 3 21 
Channel coding for LDPC  and fronthaul compression requires a significant amount of bit level processing and is 22 
well suited to a fine-grained FPGA architecture and/or low cost/power structured ASIC. Options include:  23 
• Look-aside FEC: LDPC (de)coding, Polar (de)coding, Rate (De)Matcher, (De) Interleaver, CRC, HARQ 24 
• L1 Processing: (i)FFT & CP+/-, PRACH, channel estimation & beamforming 25 
• Bump-in-wire Fronthaul: compression / decompression for latency and bandwidth reduction  26

2.2.3.3 Baseboard Management Controller 8 
BMC is used t o perform hardware power control (power on, power off and power cycle), monitor hardware status 9 
(temperatures, voltages, etc.), monitor Basic I/O System (BIOS)/ Unified Extensible Firmware Interface (UEFI) 10 
firmware status, and log system events. It provide s remote access via shared or dedicated NIC. System user can do 11 
console access via serial or physical/Kernel -based Virtual Machine (KVM). The BMC has dedicated RAM and 12 
flash memory. It provides access via serial port or Ethernet port . Figure 2-2 describes the BMC connections with 13 
related components.  14 
 15 
2.2.4 Synchronization and Timing 16 
2.2.4.1 Synchronization and Timing Design 1 17 
This section describes the synchronization and timing mechanism that is used in the O -DU8. 18 
a. Hardware Requirements 19 
The O-DU8 shall support following timing synchronization methods: 20 
1. GPS Synchronization  21 
2. Ethernet based IEEE1588v2 Synchronization 22 

 O-RAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
Copyright 2020 O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.                     
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in the Annex ZZZ  33 
 
3. BeiDou Synchronization 1 
4. BeiDou and GPS switching 2 
b. Hardware Design 3 
Depending on the timing distribution topologies used, the O-DU8 system clo ck is able to synchronize 4 
with the Grand Master Clock (GMC) using IEEE1588 via either the front haul NIC or backhaul NIC . O-5 
DU8 can also synchroniz e timing using Global Navigation Sa tellite System (GNSS). In the case of 6 
IEEE1588, the Physical Hardware Clock (PHC) within the NIC is synchronized with the GMC first, then 7 
the O-DU8 system clock is synchronized with the PHC. The O- DU8 is also capable to provide clock to 8 
the O -RU8 via front  haul if needed. When GNSS becomes available to O- DU8, it will be able to 9 
synchronize the system clock to Coordinated Universal Time (UTC). Figure  2 -10 outlines the O-DU8 10 
timing synchronization mechanisms. 11 
 12 
Figure 2-10: O-DU8 Timing Synchronization 13 
 14 
2.2.4.2 Synchronization and Timing Design 2 15 
This section describes the synchronization and timing mechanism that is used in the hardware accelerator board.  16 
a. Hardware Requirements 17 
Each hardware accelerator board that supports connectivity functions in O- DU8 must be able to support  18 
external synchronization I/O and to receive or transmit 1 PPS reference clock source in order to ensure 19 
synchronization across network.  20 
b. Hardware Design 21 
The accelerator board can operate in the master or slave mode when supporting IEEE 1588v2 22 
timing/synchronization. It can generate 1 PPS reference clock for synchronization in the master mode 23 
and can receive the 1 PPS reference clock in the slave mode for internal synchronization. The timing 24 
circuitry of the accelerator board is shown in Figure  2-11. Two FPGA SerDes transceivers are used to 25 
receive and transmit SyncE TX and RX clocks. 26

less than 400W. The total system power requirement shall be kept less than 80% of the power supply capacity.  10

2.3.2 O-RU8 Hardware Components 3 
In Section 2.3.1 , we presented many  possible O-RU8 block diagram s for reference design. In th is section, we 4 
describe their functionality, interfaces and performance of every block in the RF Processing Unit.  Since device 5 
integration is an ongoing activity, chip boundaries may be fluid and s ome functionalities may move from one 6 
block to another or entire functionalities may be absorbed into other blocks.  The sections below describe the 7 
functional blocks independent of which physical device they may reside in. 8 
2.3.2.1 Digital Processing Unit 9 
The digi tal processing unit of O- RU8 is mainly for performing tasks related to FH interface, RF interface, and 10 
OAM. 11 
i. FPGA Solution Design 1 12 
The digital processing unit of O-RU8 is mainly for performing FH interface, RF interface, and OAM functions. 13 
a. FPGA Requirement 14 
The following items are the main requirement for the O-RU8: 15 
• Interface requirement: One lane of bi -direction SerDes targeting CPRI will be @10Gbps for FH 16 
split option 8 . Four lanes of bi -direction JESD204B SerDes  will be used for 2T2R . Eight lanes of 17 
bi-direction JESD204B SerDes will be used for 4T4R 18 
• Resource requirement: FPGA resource requirements for 2T2R and 4T4R are shown in Table  2-11 19 
and Table 2-12. 20

• Speed grade, environmental requirement: -2L, 0℃ to 110℃ 5 
b. FPGA Design 6 
This solution of the digital processing unit incorporates FPGA and a processor. The FPGA handles high 7 
speed digital processing such as FH, DDC, DUC, CFR and so on. All functions are listed in the previous 8 
section. The processor is use d for hardware device configuration and the OAM function. The FPGA and 9 
the processor core can be integrated into one SoC or implemented into two devices. Here the FPGA and 10 
the processor core are integrated into one SoC device. 11

For the processor portion, the internal RAM resource may not be enough. So the external DDR is 4 
needed to let the processor handle more RAM consuming functions such as operation system or stack  5 
protocols like Network Configuration Protocol ( NetConf) Client. For the O -RU8 design, the DDR3 6 
with 256Mb*16bit memory capacity is enough. The interface between the DDR and processor can be 7 
memory interface generator ( MIG).The external Flash is used to store operation system related files, 8 
calibration information of the RF portion, NetConf related files, FPGA firmware and so on. For this O -9 
RU8 design, the flash with 2Gb memory capacity is enough. The online debug function is perfor med 10 
by external Ethernet PHY with an RJ45 connector. This allows the administrator to visit the internal 11 
function of the O -RU8 and control it. The interface between the DDR and processor can be Media -12 
Independent interface (MII). The Ethernet PHY device can  be very general 100M Eth ernet 13 
Transceiver. The FPGA has one SerDes lane connected to optical module to perform the fronthaul link 14 
between O-RU8 and O-DU8 /FHGW8. Another 4 SerDes lanes of the FPGA are needed to connect one 15 
transceiver of the O -RU8 to tran smit or receive IQ sample by the interface of JESD204B while the 16 
FPGA needs synchronized clock signals to work well. The interface between PLL and FPGA should 17 
be Low-Voltage Differential Signaling (LVDS). 18 
2.3.2.2 RF Processing Unit 19 
For the RF processing unit of O -RU8, it will perform functionalities of ADC, DAC, LO, down converter, up 20 
converter and etc. 21 
2.3.2.2.1 Transceiver Reference Design  22 
For the O-RU8 the sampling function and frequency convers ion function can be performed by transceiver. The 23 
purpose of using the transc eiver is to saving power and size of the PCB.  The Transceiver is to convert between 24

high speed baseband data and a low -level RF for both transmit and receive signal chain.  In add ition, the 1 
transceiver is responsible for orchestration of control signals no t limited to the PA enable, LNA enable, LNA 2 
bypass as well as other required system level signals.   3 
a. Hardware Requirements 4 
Include the requirements for this component. 5 
• Interface Requirements: The interface requirements for the Transceivers are list in Table 2-13. 6 
Table 2-13: Interface requirements of the Transceiver 7 
Item Name Description 
High Speed Data 
 
High speed data represents the baseband information being transmitte d or received.  
Depending on the configuration of the O -RAN device, various bandwidths may be 
supported leading to a range of payload rates.  Options for data include parallel data paths, 
JESD204B and JESD204C.  Up to 8 lanes in each direction may be supported although 
fewer is preferred.  Options such as DPD a nd numeric precision will impact the payload 
rate.  Several options are shown in the following table .  All data represents IQ 16 -bit 
(N=16) precision.  Some devices support IQ 12 bit (N’=12) which may  reduce the required 
data rates accordingly.  The table s below assume 1 ORx for 2 TRx.  From Table 2-14 and 
Table 2-15, the number of lanes required may be determined by dividing the total bit rates 
shown b y the capacity of a lane, typically 12.5 GBPS for JESD204B and 25 GBPS for 
JESD204C.   
Reference Clock 
(Device Clock) 
 
The transceiver should receive a reference for internal clock and LO synthesis needs.  This 
reference clock can function as the JESD204 Device Clock where the interface is by 
SERDES.  The specific clock frequency is determined by the operati on mode of the 
transceiver and may range from 1Hz upward. 
SYSREF 
 
If the transceiver supports SERDES, then it should accept a SYSREF signal from the c lock 
or data source as appropriate.  The number and configuration for the SYSREF is 
dependent on the operating mode of the transceiver. 
SYNCB 
 
If the transceiver supports SERDES, then it should also support a SYNCB for each link as 
appropriate.   
Control 
 
Control of the transceiver is by way of 3 or 4 wire SPI or IIC functioning as a slave.  
Support for 1.8V control is required and tolerance of 3.3V is preferred.  The transceiver 
may optionally include a separate SPI master for control of peripheral devices as required.  
GPIO 
 
The transceiver may optionally include GPIO for controlling peripherals includi ng but not 
limited to PAs, LNAs and other devices.  These GPIOs should at a minimum support 1.8V 
outputs but the specifics will be determined by the conne cted devices.  The GPIO should 
also support input from peripheral devices.  Input should at a minimum support 1.8V logic 
with tolerance of 3.3V preferred. 
Tx Enable 
 
The transceiver should provide an output to support enabling and disabling the external 
devices in the transmit chain such as a TxVGA (optional) and PA.   
 
Rx Enable The transceiver should p rovide an output to support enabling and disabling the external 
devices in the transmit chain such as a RF Front End Module or LNA.   
LNA Bypass The transceiver should provide an output to support bypassing the LNA appropriately in 
the condition of a large blocker if so required.

Copyright 2020 O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.                     
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in the Annex ZZZ  47 
 
RF Outputs RF outputs including the main Tx signal should support 50 ohm or 100 ohms signalling.  
These outputs can be either single ended or differential. 
RF Inputs RF inputs including the main Rx and the Observation Rx (ORx) (for DPD) should support 
50 ohm or 100 ohms signalling.  These inputs can be either single ended or differential.  
The device should support at least 1 ORx.

• Rx1 through Rx4 are differential receiver inputs to their respective cores. 1 
• Tx1 through Tx4 are differential transmitter output from their respective cores. 2 
• ORx1 through ORx4 are differential observation receiver inputs. 3 
• SCLK is the serial control clock input. 4 
• CSB is the active low device select for the control interface. 5 
• SDO is the serial data output for the control interface.  Th is pin may be omitted for 3 -pin control 6 
implementations. 7 
• SDIO is a bidirectional serial data interface.  In 4 -pin mode, this pin functions as the serial data 8 
input. 9 
• GPIO1 – GPIO# are general purpose IO signals used for interrupts, enables, test mode and resets.  10 
These are used as interface and control for peripheral devices including controllers, TxVGAs, PA, 11 
LNA and similar devices where SPI control is too slow. 12 
• SYNCIN are differential pins associated with the receiver channels of the JESD204 interface.  In  13 
not used, they are typically grounded.  Up to 4 pair may be supported. 14 
• SerDes Out are differential JESD204B/C data output interfaces.  Up to 8 lanes may be active.  15 
• SerDes In are differential JESD204B/C data input interfaces.  Up to 8 lanes may be active.  16 
• SYNCOUT are differential pins associated with the transmitter ch annels of the JESD204 interface.  17 
If not used, do not connect.  Up to 4 pair may be supported. 18 
2.3.2.2.2 Power Amplifier (PA) Reference Design  19 
The Power Amplifier boosts the RF output to the level required for the base station class. 20 
a. Hardware Requirements 21 
The PA should have large enough gain to reduce the need for an additional driver.  This will reduce cost 22 
and PCB space. The output power should be at least 27dBm (30dBm for 500mW/port to compensate for  23 
the loss of switch and antenna filter). The ACLR should be greater than 47dBc according to the related 24 
3GPP test mode. DPD is needed to reduce the power consumption. The P1 dB requirement is closely 25 
related to the DPD algorithm. 26

Table 2-18: Interface requirements of the transmit PA 5 
Item Name Description 
Enable 
 
The enable input should be compatible with 1.8V logic and tolerate 3.3V as required.  A 
logic high enables the PA .  A logic low disables the device and places it in a minimum 
dissipation mode. 
RF Outputs 
 
RF outputs support 50 -ohm single ended to properly  interface to a directional coupler, 
isolator, switch or antenna. 
RF Inputs RF inputs should support 50 ohm, single ended match to the transceiver output or preamp. 
RF power detector Power detector (optional) 
 6 
• Power Dissipation: Dissipation while enabled should not exceed ~3W. 7 
 8 
b. Hardware Design 9 
RFin is the input to the PA, RF out is the output of the PA. V cc and Vbias are the power inputs of the PA. 10 
PAEN is the control pin to disable or enable the PA. The input and output should match to 50 ohm as 11 
much as possible to reduce the reflection. V cc and Vbias need capacitors to reduce the DC power ripples 12 
and give a short route to reduce the RF energy leakage. Figure 2-25 shows the details. 13

2.3.2.2.3 Low Noise Amplifier (LNA) Reference Design  1 
The purpose of the LNA is to boost the Rx signal to a level that can nominally interface directly to the transceiver.  2 
This block will typically be a 2-stage amplifier with a 2nd stage bypass.  The frontend will also include a TR switch 3 
to shunt any Tx signal to a termination away from the amplifier.   4

• One stage LNA 4 
RFin is the input port of the LNA. RFout is the output of th e LNA. Vcc is the power input of the LNA. 5 
LNA EN is the control pin to disable or enable the LNA. The input match to 50 ohm as much as 6 
possible to reduce the reflection. But for output of the LNA,  it is hard to get best Noise figure  and the 7 
output return loss. Compromise is needed between NF and return loss. Usually the return loss should be 8 
around -10dB or -12dB. Also is should be very careful to assure the stability of the LNA in a large 9 
bandwidth. Vcc needs capacitors to reduce the DC power ripples and give a short route to reduce the 10 
RF energy leakage. Figure 2-26 shows the details. 11

2.3.2.2.4 RF Switch Reference Design  3 
For TDD use, the TX and R X links work spiritedly by time duplex. The switch is used to change the RF link 4 
according to the TDD duplex. In the TDD TX mode, the switch is switched to connect PA and antenna. In TDD 5 
RX mode, the switch is switched to LNA and antenna. 6 
a. Hardware Requirements 7

RF Inputs The port connected to the PA. 
RF inputs should support 50 ohm, single ended to match the PA output.

2.3.3 Synchronization and Timing 14 
The purpose of the Clocking device is to accept the network reference clock, typically 1 PPS, and generate a jitter 15 
free reference clock(s) for other devices in the system including the RF transceiver and digital block.  The clock is 16 
typically part of an I EEE 1588 implementation either controlled by an external stack or implemented in the clock 17 
device itself which could be part of the baseband implementation.  18 


 O-RAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
Copyright 2020 O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.                     
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in the Annex ZZZ  59 
 
a. Hardware Requirements 1 
Hardware requirements are: 2 
• Interface Requirements: The interface requirement s of the transmit Clocking are listed in  3 
Table 2-24. 4 
Table 2-24: Interface requirements of the clocking 5 
Item Name Description 
Reference Clock 
Input 
 
The clock device should receive a network reference clock from the FPGA/ASIC.  This 
typically could be a 1pps, 10 MHz or other  standard reference as determined by the 
specific implementation.  More than one input is allowed that may be selected between 
when the reliability of one reference is comp romised.  Standard differential clocking 
should be used to preserve signal integrity. 
Control 
 
Control of the transceiver is by way of 3 or 4 wire SPI or IIC functioning as a slave.  
Support for 1.8V control is required and tolerance of 3.3V is preferred. Typically, the 
clock devices will be part of an IEEE1588v2 protocol loop controlled by way of this 
control interface or other GPIO lines as required by the hardware implementation.  
Clock Outputs 
 
One or more clock outputs are required to drive the digital device and transceiver clock 
inputs.  Each output should be independently programm able in frequency to suit the 
application.  The outputs should nominally be differential to preserve clocking edge and to 
maximize tolerance of high common mode signals. 
RF Inputs RF inputs should support 50 ohm or 100 ohms, single ended or differential s ignalling to 
match the transceiver output.

• Power Dissipation:  Total dissipation should be about less than 2W. 7

b. Hardware Design 1 
For the clocking function, it is usually performed by a  synchronization IC which may include one or more 2 
PLLs. And it also can supply numbers of output port to support different frequency clocking.   3 
 4 
Figure 2-30: PLL Reference Design Diagram 5 
REFA and REFB are dif ferential reference clock inputs from the source to be cleaned up and used as the 6 
system reference.  REF_SEL is the control pin used to select between REFA and REFB signals .  LF1 is 7 
PLL1 external loop filter. VCXO_VT is the VCXO control voltage.  This pin is connected to the voltage 8 
control pin of the external VCXO. VCXO_IN are differential signals from the external VCXO.  These 9 
typically can be con figured for single ended operation as well.  LF2_CAP is the external loop filter 10 
capacitor for loop 2.  This c ap is connected between this pin and LDO_VCO pin. LDO_VCO is the on -11 
chip LDO regular decoupling for the VCO.   RESET(bar) is an active low pin to reset the internal logic t o 12 
their default states. CS(bar) is an active low chip select for serial control. SCLK/SCL is a serial control 13 
port clock for both SPI and I2C.  SDIO/SDA is a bidirectional serial data pin for both SPI and I2C. SDO 14 
is the serial data out pin for 4 -wire mode. OUT0 through OUT13 are differential programmable output 15 
clock signals.  These are the primary outputs of this device and provide high performance clock signals to 16 
the transceiver, baseband device and other key elements.  This device is also responsible f or providing 17 
SYSREF to various devices in the system if required.  It may also use an external SYSREF as a source 18 
that may be retimed for local timing if necessary. STATUS0/SP0 is a lock detect and other status signal. 19 
STATIS1/SP1 is a lock detect and other status signal. SYSREF_IN is an external SYSREF input clock is 20 
a differential input representing the JESD204 synchronization from the external source.  21

2.3.6 Power Unit 1 
For Picocell, for the power solution of the O -RU8 can be over Ethernet cable (cat5E, cat6A) by POE (POE+, 2 
POE++) or directly power cable with fibre. And for the board power solution, LDO and DCDC can be used. 3 
a. Hardware Requirements 4 
For PA unit, it should have larger enough gain to reduce extra driver amplifier for cost and PCB space. 5 
The output power should be at least 27dBm count on the lo ss of switch and antenna filter. The ACLR 6 
should larger than 47dBc according to the related 3GPP test mode. To reduce the power consumption, 7 
DPD is needed. And the P1 dB requirement is closely related to the DPD algorithm. 8

2.6.1 Digital Processing Unit 2

function. 4 
i. FPGA Solution Design 1 5

• Interface requirement 9 
Table 2-30: Interface requirements of the FPGA 10 
Item Name Description 
SerDes lane 
At least 8 lanes of bi-direction10Gb SerDes

 11 
b. FPGA Design 12 
This unit handles multiple high speed interfaces and the digital signal processing. The main chipset is based on 13 
FPGA with ARM multi -core processor while FPGA is responsible for high speed data processing and ARM 14 
cores are mainly for configuration and management.  15 
The FPGA function is shown below: 16 
For the uplink processing, the CPRI interface module is used to receiver CPRI link form O -RU8 and generate 17 
the reference frequency and time slot for time and frequency synchronization. Then the CPRI de -frame 18 
module get the uplink IQ bit stream of the  time domain the carrier from O -RU8. If the uplink IQ stream is 19 
compressed, then it needs the de-compression module to translate them into original bit width.  20 
After that all the O -RU8 uplink IQ streams are combined together and the precondition for the co mbine 21 
function is that each stream from different O-RU8 are aligned in time domain. 22 
If the FHGW 8 can support two separate cells then the two cell uplink signal should be aggregated or 23 
interleaved together. Then capture the IQ streams in the CPRI frame and transmitted by CPRI interface to the 24 
O-DU8. 25 
For the downlink processing, the procedure is much the same with uplink but with an inverse sequence. The 26 
difference is that the downlink signal is duplicated with 8 copies and sends to different O -RU8. 27 
There is also a CPRI OAM module; actually this module will work with the processor (Arm) to accommodate 28 
the OAM of different O-RU8 and the FHGW8 itself. 29 

 O-RAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
Copyright 2020 O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V.                     
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in the Annex ZZZ  68

2.6.5 Power 2 
At minimum, fully describe the power consumptions for this white box.  Include all AC/DC input or outputs and 3 
their ratings. Not mandatory but if you like you c an add summary of all component's power requirement and 4 
overall white box. 5 
a. Hardware Requirements 6 
• Power requirement:  The power solution is divided into two parts.  7 
1. Input power module:  This power module must support AC to DC conversion. Usually it 8 
converts 220V AC to 48V DC. 9 
2. Output power module: It will supply power to the O -RU8 connected to the FHGW8 and also 10 
the device on the FHGW8 itself. 11 
 12 
Table 2-34: Requirements of the power unit 13 
Item Description 
Input voltage AC:100V~264V 
Output 
voltage 
DC: 48V  
Output power At least 600W 
b. Hardware Design 14 
Describe hardware reference design for this power unit. 15 
This unit has two main functions which are internal power supply and external/remote power supply. The 16 
input power is normally AC (100V to 250V) but DC input could be optional. For remote power supply, it 17 
will support -48V DC via either standalone cable or cat-6A cable. 18 
Usually a separated AC -DC power supply is used for the 220V to 48V conversion. It is very common 19 
power supply on the market. 20 
It should have fan to cool itself and handle for easy plug in and out. 21 
Then the output power is divided into two por tions. One for O -RU8 power supply, the other is for the 22 
device on the FHGW8 board. 23 
For the on -board power solution, 48V to 12V converter is needed as standard 1/8 brick module. Then the 24 
power voltage is further changer to lower level such as 5V, 3.3V, 1.1V , 1.0V and so on by DC/DC or LDO 25 
devices. 26

Annex 2 CPRI specification reference design 1 
A2.1  Introductory Material 2 
A2.1.1 Scope 3 
This specification is used to be as a reference design for the CPRI specification. The content of this 4 
specification is to specify the parameters of the ‘Common Public Radio Interface (CPRI); Interface Specification ’ 5 
[1]. This includes User Plane data and Control and Managem ent Plane transport mechanisms.  This specification 6 
supports the Option 8 split fronthaul connection between O -DU8 and O -RU8(s). For  indoor Pico use case, this 7 
specification also supports FHGW8 between O-DU8 and O-RU8. 8 
 9 
A2.1.2 References  10 
The following documents contain provisions which, through reference in this text, constitute provisions of the 11 
present document. 12 
[1] Ericsson AB, Huawei Technologies Co. Ltd, NEC Corporation, Alcatel Lucent and Nokia Networks, 13 
‘Common Public Radio Interface (CPRI); Interface Specification’. 14 
http://www.cpri.info/downloads/CPRI_v_7_0_2015-10-09.pdf 15 
[2] ORAN-WG4.MP.0-v02.00: O-RAN Fronthaul Working Group Management Plane Specification, Release 16 
02.00, August 2019. 17 
A2.1.2.1 Definitions and Abbreviations 18 
A2.1.2.1.1 Definitions 19 
Antenna-carrier (AxC):  One antenna-carrier is the amount of digital baseband (IQ) U -plane data necessary for 20 
either reception or transmission of only one carrier at one independent antenna element. 21

defined in the present document takes precedence over the definition of the same abbreviation, if any, a s in [2]. 24

A2.1.4 Supported RAN Standard 3 
This specification supports 3GPP NR R15 related requirement. 4 
A2.1.5 Transmission Distance 5 
This specification defines minimum transmission distance between REC and REs. 6 
For indoor Pico use case, the minimum transmission distance between O -DU8 and FHGW8 should be larger than 7 
2km. 8 
For indoor P ico use case, the minimum transmission distance between  FHGW8 and O-RU8 should be larger than 9 
200m. 10 
 11 
A2.1.6 System Topology 12

For indoor use case: 14 
This specification should support star connection. The FHGW8 should support star connection with O-RU8. 15 
 16 
2. Chain topology 17 
For indoor use case: 18 
This specification should support chain connection. At least 2 cascad ed stages should be supported for 19 
FHGW8. 20

The interface with 24330.24Mbps line rate contains 192 AxC. 23 
 24

A3.1.3  Definitions and Abbreviations 42 
A3.1.3.1 Definitions 43 

 ORAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
© 2020 O-RAN Alliance  All Rights Reserved 
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ  
89 
 
For the purposes of the present document, the terms and definitions given in 3GPP TR  21.905 [1] and the following 1 
apply. A term defined in the present document takes precedence over the definition of the same term, if any, in 3GPP 2 
TR 21.905 [1]. 3 
Antenna Line: connection between O-RU8 and antenna 4 
C-Plane: Control Plane: refers specifically to real -time control between O-DU8 and O-RU8, and should not be confused 5 
with the UE’s control plane 6 
c_eAxC: component eAxC: a portion of an eAxC flow assigned to a specific O-DU8 processing element.  7 
DL: DownLink: data flow towards the radiating antenna (generally on the LLS interface)  8 
eAxC: extended Antenna -Carrier: a data flow for a single antenna (or spatial stream) for a single carrier in a single 9 
sector. 10 
M-Plane: Management Plane: refers to non-real-time management operations between the O-DU8 and the O-RU8 11 
NMS: A Network Management System dedicated to O-RU8 operations 12 
Port: End of a transport link – in most cases this is an optical port 13 
Port Number: A number which identifie s a port (see Port). In case of SFP/SFP+ port, port number value is 0 to N -1 14 
where N is number of ports in the device. Numbers 0 to N -1 are assigned to ports in order following order of labels on 15 
the device (labels for ports are not necessarily numbers starting from zero) 16 
O-RU8 Controller: A network function that is permitted to control the configuration of an O -RU8. Examples of O-RU8 17 
controllers include, an O-DU8, a classical NMS, or other network automation platforms.  18 
S-Plane: Synchronization Plane: refers to traffic between the O-RU8 or O-DU8 to a synchronization controller. 19 
Spatial stream: the data flow on the DL associated with precoded data (may be same as layers or different if there is 20 
expansion in the precoding), and on UL associated with the numbe r of outputs from the digital beamforming 21 
(sometimes called “beams”).  22 
SSM: Synchronization Status Message: part of ITU G.781 and G.8264 standards. 23 
TRX: Refers to the specific processing chain in an O -RU8 associated with D/A or A/D converters.  Due to digi tal 24 
beamforming the number of TRXs may exceed the number of spatial streams, and due to analog beamforming the 25 
number of TRXs may be lower than the number of antenna elements. 26 
U-Plane: User Plane: refers to IQ sample data transferred between O-DU8 and O-RU8 27 
UL: UpLink: data flow away from the radiating antenna 28 
Virtual Connection: a connection between O-RU8 and O-RU8 controller. This connection is established by means of 29 
autodetection procedure and is supervised by supervision procedure. 30 
A3.1.3.2 Abbreviations 31 
For the purposes of the present document, the abbreviations given in 3GPP TR  21.905 [1] and the following apply. An 32 
abbreviation defined in the present document takes precedence over the definition of the same abbreviation, if any, in 33

figures.  33

A3.2.1.2 M-Plane architecture model 9 
A NETCONF/YANG based M -Plane is used for supporting the management features including “start up” installation, 10 
software management, configuration management, performance management, fault management and file management 11 
towards the O-RU8. The M-Plane supports two architectural models: 12 
1. Hierarchical model .  As shown on the left side Figure  1, the O -RU8 is managed entirely by on e or more  O-13 
DU8(s) using a NETCONF based M -Plane interface. When the O -RU8 is managed by multiple O-DU8(s), it is 14 
typically for enabli ng O-DU8 and/or transport connectivity redundancy capabilities. Refer to Chapter 3 for more 15 
details. 16 
2. Hybrid model. As shown on the right side of Figure 1, the hybrid architecture enables one or more direct logical 17 
interface(s) between management system(s) and O -RU8 in addition to a logical interface between O -DU8 and the  18 
O-RU8. It should be noted that the NETCONF clients connecting to the O -RU8 may be of different classes (e.g.  19 
O-DU8 and NMS). For example , functions like O -RU8 software management, performance management, 20 
configuration management and fault management can be managed directly by the management system(s).  21 
In the hybrid model, the O -RU8 has end to end IP layer connectivity with the NMS. From a physical network point of 22 
view, this connectivity could be via the O-DU8, where the O-DU8 is acting as an IP/Ethernet packet forwarder, forwards 23 
the packets between O -RU8 and the NMS. Direct l ogical communication between an O -RU8 and NMS can be enabled 24 
via O-RU8s being assigned routable IPs or local private IPs resolved by a NAT function in the network (or implemented 25 
at the O -DU8). Refer to chapter 3 for details how O -RU8 acquires the IP address of O -DU8 and NMS for the M -plane 26 
communication. 27 

 ORAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
© 2020 O-RAN Alliance  All Rights Reserved 
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ  
92 
 
As described in Chapter 3, there is no explicit signaling to indicate that an O -RU8 is operating in a hierarchical or 1 
hybrid configuration. All NETCONF servers supporting this M -Plane specification shall s upport multiple NETCONF 2 
sessions, and hence all complaint O-RU8s shall be able to support both hierarchical and hybrid deployment. 3 
 4 
Figure Annex 3-2: M-Plane Architecture 5 
NETCONF/YANG is used as the network element management protocol [3] and  data modeling language  [4]. Use of 6 
such a standardized framework and  common modeling language simplifies integration between O -DU8 and O-RU8 as 7 
well a s operator network integration  (in terms of running service) in case of elements sharing a common set of 8 
capabilities. The framework supports integration of products with dif fering capabilities enabled by well -defined 9 
published data models. NETCONF also na tively supports a hybrid architecture which enables multiple clients to 10 
subscribe and receive information originating at the NETCONF server in the O-RU8. 11 
A3.2.1.3 Transport Network 12 
Based on the transport topology, various modes of network connectivity are possible between O -RU8 and O-DU8 and 13 
NMS.  14 
The basic requirement for M-Plane is to have end to end IP connectivity between the O -RU8 and the elements managing 15 
it ( O-DU8, NMS, or so called “O -RU8 Controllers”). The connectivity between the O -DU8 and NMS and its 16 
management plane are not in scope of this specification. IPv4 shall be supported as a mandatory transport protocol for 17 
M-Plane and IPv6 support is optional . The M-Plane shall be NAT agnostic for the O -RU8 to O-DU8/NMS connectivity, 18 
in order to support a variety of possible network and IP addressing deployments.  19 
A3.2.1.4 M-Plane functional description 20 
The M -Plane provides the following major functionalities to the O -RU8. These features are implemented using the 21 
NETCONF provided functions. 22 
 “Start up” installation  23 


 ORAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
© 2020 O-RAN Alliance  All Rights Reserved 
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ  
93 
 
During startup, the O -RU8 acquires its network layer parameters either via static (pre- configured in the O -RU8) or 1 
dynamically via DHCP or DHCPv6. During this process it also acquires the IP address of the O -RU8 controller(s), and 2 
establishes the NETCONF connectivity using the “call home” feature. The capability exchange is performed between 3 
the client and server as part of the initial NETCONF Hello exchanges. Details of these steps are provided in chapter 3.  4 
SW management  5 
The M-Plane is responsible for software download, installation, validation and activation of new SW when requested by 6 
O-RU8 Controller. The software download is triggered by NETCONF RPC procedures, and the actual software package 7 
download is done using sFTP. In this version of the M -Plane specification, sFTP is the only defined file transfer 8 
protocol for software and file management. 9 
Configuration management 10 
Configuration management covers various scenarios li ke Retrieve Resource State, Modify Resource State, Modify 11 
Parameters and Retrieve Para meters. NETCONF get -config and edit-config RPCs shall be used for configuration 12 
parameter retrieval and updates at the O-RU8  13 
Performance management 14 
Performance management describes the measurements and counters used to collect data related to O -RU8 operations. 15 
The purpose of Performance Management is optimizing the operation of the O-RU8.  16 
The measurement results are reported by two options:  17 
1. YANG Notification : This optio n uses the stats definition of YANG model per measurement group. In this 18 
case, get rpc and/or notification will be used (see Chapter 7 for more details).  19 
2. File Upload: This option uses the file upload procedure defined in File management. The measurement r esults 20 
are saved to a data file periodically.  21 
Fault Management 22 
Fault management is re sponsible for sending alarm notifications to the NETCONF Client. F ault Management allows 23 
alarm notifications to be disabled or enabled as well as alarm subscription. 24 
File Management 25 
File management allows the O -RU8 Controller to trigger an O -RU8 to perform upload of fil es stored on O -RU8 to O-26 
RU8 Controller. The O -RU8 may provide different kinds of files and retrieved files can be used f or various purposes.  27 
Simultaneous multiple file upload operations can be supported under the same sFTP connection between O -RU8 to O-28 
DU8/NMS. 29 
A3.2.2 Interfaces 30 
The M-Plane interface is defined between the O -DU8/NMS and the O-RU8. The protocol stack of the M -Plane interface 31 
is shown in Figure Annex 3-3. The transport network layer is built on IP transport and  TCP/SSH is used to carry the M -32 
Plane message between the DU/NMS and the O-RU8. 33

A3.2.3 YANG Module Introduction 3 
The data models representing the M -Plane are organized as a set of reusable YANG modules. It is also the intent to 4 
reuse the publicly available and generic YANG models as much as possible instead of developing customized O -RAN 5 
specific modules. Refer to th e various chapters, Annex D and t he repository of YANG models for more details on each 6 
of these modules.  7 
A3.2.4 Security 8 
The M-Plane provides end to end security as a mandatory feature. The security is provided using the SSHv2 layer in 9 
accordance with RFC 6242  [5]. RFC 6242 provides the procedu re for interoperability with NETCONF 10 
implementations. If there are multiple NETCONF sessions established with a single O -RU8, each session should be 11 
established over a separate SSH tunnel. 12 
Plane Integrity (protection 
from modifications) 
Confidentiality 
(encryption 
protection) 
Authentication 
(validity of the 
originator) 
Remarks 
M-Plane Yes Yes Yes Using the SSHv2 layer used 
for NETCONF transport 
Table Annex 3-1: M-Plane Security 13 
SSHv2 performs server host authentication, key exchange, encryption, and integrity protection. It also derives a unique 14 
session ID that may be used by higher -level protocols. The end point authentication sho uld be done as per RFC 4252 15 
[6]. Chapter 3 describes the authentication approach based on username and password as well as based on X.509 16 
certificates. 17 
The SSHv2 transport level security (encryption algor ithms, data integrity algorithms) shall be based on RFC4253 [7]. 18 
As per this RFC, 3des -cbc shall be the mandatory cyphering protocol, and rest of the ones listed as optional. For data 19 
integrity, hmac-sha1 shall be the mandatory algorithm, and rest of the l isted algorithms shall be optional. Public key -20 
based host authentication shall be used for authenticating the server (RFC 4253) by the clients, and username/password 21 
based client authentication shall be done by the server as part of the SSH session establi shment.  22 
As an additional option, both client and se rver may implement authentication based on X.509 certificates. With this 23 
option, RSA 2048 bit shall be supported for the Public Key algorithm, aes -cbc shall be supported for the cyphering 24 
algorithm and hmac-sha256 shall be supported for integrity algorithm. 25

Note: FIPS-186-3 introduces longer keys but as only FIPS/186 -2 is referred in RFC4253 and SHA -1 is used, then key 1 
length defined in FIPS-186-2 shall be supported. 2 
A3.3 “Start up” installation  3 
A3.3.1 CPRI Start-up Sequence 4 
This section defines the sequence of actions to be performed by master and slave ports connected by CPRI. 5 
When both the slave port and the master port are in state F or G, the link is in normal operation . 6 
The start-up procedure accomplishes two main things: 7 
 Synchronization of layer 1: byte alignment and hyper frame alignment 8 
 Alignment of capabilities of the master and slave ports: line bit rate, protocol, C&M channel bit rate,  9 
 C&M protocol, vendor specific signalling 10 
Refer to ‘Common Public Radio Interface (CPRI); Interface Specification’ [35] ，section 4.2.7.9, Section 4.5 for detail. 11 
 12 
Figure Annex 3-4: Start-up Sequence 13 
Appointment: 14 
- In State D: bit2-bit0 of Z.66.0 must be set to 0 which means Ethernet is used for CPRI M plane channel. 15 
- Use fast C&M channel to transmit O-RAN Management Plane messages. 16 
- After RE enter State F (Operation), O-DU8 and O-RU8 should begin to setup Management Plane. 17

 2

- Physical interface(s) is(are) connected. 7 
Post-condition:   8 
- O-RU8 is ready for the radio transmission to the air on at least one carrier if packet transmission received from O -9 
DU8  10

- At least one O -RU8 Controller/NETCONF client with “super -user” access privileges can control any operation to 12 
the O-RU8/NETCONF server in O-RU8. 13

 ORAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00

At the power-on of O-RU8 or following an O-RU8 restart, the following procedures are performed. 1 
1. O-RU8 performs transport layer resolution (DHCP, MAC, VLAN, IP, etc.) and  recovers IP address(es) of O -RU8 2 
controller(s). 3 
2. O-RU8 begins synchronization of the O-RU8 against a Primary Reference Clock. (Note: step 2 may be in parallel with 4 
step 1 for some O-RU8 implementation.) 5 
3. O-RU8 performs NETCONF Call Home to O-RU8 controller(s). 6 
4. O-RU8 controller performs SSH connection establishment. 7 
5. O-RU8 and O-RU8 controller perform NETCONF capability discovery. 8 
6. O-RU8 controller performs optional provisioning of new management accounts (typically only performed once 9 
during pre-staging) 10 
7. O-RU8 and O-RU8 controller perform supervision of NETCONF connection. 11 
8. O-RU8 controller performs retrieval of O-RU8 information. 12 
9. O-RU8 controller performs SW management. 13 
10. O-RU8 controller recovers the O -RU8 delay profile from the O -RU8 and configuration of U -plane transport 14 
connectivity between O-DU8 and O-RU8. 15 
11. The O -RU8 controller con figures transport connectivity checking on the O -RU8, enabling the O-DU8 to perform  16 
U-Plane transport connectivity checking between O-DU8 and O-RU8. 17 
12. O-RU8 controller performs Fault Management activation. 18 
13. O-RU8 controller activates performance measurement (if required at start-up timing). 19 
14. O-RU8 controller retrieves O-RU8 state, including synchronization information, from O-RU8. 20 
15. O-RU8 controller configures the O-RU8 operational parameters. 21 
16. Service available. 22 
Note, the synchronization procedures started in step 2 needs to be completed before service is availab le. 23 
 24 
This chapter mainly covers 1, 3 to 7 and 11 as sub-sections. 25 
Cross Reference of other chapters: 26 
The detail of 2. Synchronization management is described in Chapter 10. 27 
The method of 8 and 14 retrieval of O-RU8 information is described in Chapter 6. 28 
The detail of 9. SW management is described in Chapter 5. 29 
The detail of 10. U-plane configuration is described in Chapter 12.  30 
The detail of 13. Performance management is described in Chapter 7. 31 
The detail of 12. Fault management is described in Chapter 8. 32 
The method of 15. Control to make service available is described in Chapter 12. 33 
A3.3.3 Management Plane Transport aspects 34 
This sub-section provides the M -plane transport establishment scenario between O-RU8 and O-RU8 controller(s), such 35 
as O-DU8 and/or NMS. The transport layer address of M- plane is only the target in this section. Transport  aspects of the 36 
C plane and U plane are covered in Chapter 4. 37 

 ORAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
© 2020 O-RAN Alliance  All Rights Reserved 
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ  
99 
 
Pre-condition:  1 
- Physical interface is connected. 2 
- The NETCONF serv er and NETCONF Client(s) have an identical NETCONF call home port con figured, to ensure 3 
the NETCONF client listens on the same port used by the NETCONF Server. 4 
Post-condition:   5 
- Transport Layer address(es) for M-plane are known to O-RU8 and controllers.  6 
- O-RU8 is aware of the physical port(s) for M-plane, e.g., if there are multiple ports in the O-RU8. 7 
- O-RU8 is aware of the VLAN(s) to be used for M-Plane, e.g., if VLANs are used in the transport network. 8 
- Then O-RU8 is ready to establish TCP connection for NETCONF call home. 9 
For the transport establishment, there are the following alternatives: 10 
a) Manual transport layer ad dress configuration in O -RU8. This configuration contains the addresses for O -RU8 and 11 
NETCONF client(s). The method to manually con figure the O -RU8 is out of scope in this specification.  Assuming 12 
manual configuration is successful, the NETCONF server shall  be able to recover this con figured information and use 13 
the o-ran-mplane-int.yang model to communicate this operational-state to a NETCONF client. 14 
b) DHCP server provides O -RU8’s transport layer address information together with the identity of the NETCONF 15 
client. This identity encodes either the  transport layer address or FQDN of the NETCONF client. If an FQDN is 16 
signaled, the O -RU8 shall use the DNS server address provided by the DHCP server to recover the IP address 17 
corresponding to FQDN of the NETCONF client. 18 
c) If IPv6 is supported, Stateless Address Auto -Configuration (SLAAC) is used to con figure the O-RU8’s transport 19 
address with the DHCP server providing the identity of the NETCONF client. This identity encodes either the  transport 20 
layer address or F QDN of the NETCONF client. If an FQDN is signaled, the O -RU8 shall use the DNS server address 21 
provided by the DHCP server to recover the IP address corresponding to FQDN of the NETCONF client.  22 
Note, a NETCONF client can determine whether an O -RU8 supports IPv6 by using the get  rpc to recover the list of 23 
interfaces supported by the O -RU8 and using the presence of the augmented i pv6 container in the o -ran-interfaces-cpri 24 
module to indicate IPv6 is supported. 25 
The O-RU8 uses the o-ran-dhcp.yang model to be able to expose information signaled by the DHCP server. 26

A3.3.3.1.1 O-RU8 identification in DHCP 6 
The O-RU8 shall identify itself to DHCP ser vers by u sing DHCP option(s)  using the vendor -class-data string within 7 
the o-ran-dhcp YANG model. For DHCPv4, there are two alternatives. One uses option 60 Vendor Class Identifier, 8 
RFC2132 [8]. The other uses option 124 Vendor Identifying Vendor Class Opt ion, RFC3 925 [9]. The O -RU8 shall 9 
support at least one of these options. If the O -RU8 supports IPv6, then it shall identify itself using the DHCPv6 Vendor 10 
Class Option. 11 
A3.3.3.2 DHCPv4 Vendor Class Option: 12 
• Option: 60 13 
• Vendor Class Identifier Option 60: string  14 
The format of the vendor class string shall be configured to one of the following three options:  15 
1. ““o-ran-ru2/<vendor>, e.g., “o-ran-ru2/vendorA 16 
2. “o-ran-ru2/<vendor>/<product-code>”, e.g., “o-ran-ru2/vendorA/ORUAA100 17 
3. “o-ran-ru2/<vendor>/<product-code>/<serial-number>”,e.g. , “o-ran-18 
ru2/vendorA/ORUAA100/FR1918010111 19

• Option: 124 21 
• Enterprise number: O-RAN-alliance 53148 22

DHCPv6 Vendor Class Option: 24 
• Option: 16 25 
• Enterprise number: O-RAN-alliance 53148 26 
• Vendor-Class-Data: the format of the string shall follow the rules defined for the DHCPv4 Vendor Class Option  27 
The DHCP Server may use this information when allocating IP addresses or when configuring management plane O -28 
RU8 Controller(s) information in the O-RU8. 29 
A3.3.3.3 Management Plane VLAN Discovery Aspects 30 
The O-RU8 will be connected to one or more Ethernet ports  for M plane. The transport systems may be realiz ed such 31 
that these Ethernet ports may be con figured either as an access port, where untagged Ethernet frames are used, or as a 32 
trunk port, where multiple VLANs  are con figured. At start up, the O -RU8 will typically not be able to immediately 33 
determine whether its ports are attached to remote transport equipment configured for access or trunk mode operation.   34 
Once an O-RU8 completes its boot -up sequence and Ether net connectivity for M plane is detected on at least one of its 35 
Ethernet interfaces, the O-RU8 starts management plane connection establishment. 36 
The O-RU8 needs to determine whether it is connected to an access port or a trunk port. In particular, when con nected to 37 
a trunk port, the O -RU8 needs to additionally determine the VLAN identity/ies used to s upport the management plane 38 
communication(s). The VLAN(s) used to support management plane communications can be identified by the DHCP 39 
server replying to the DHCP DISCOVER message, as described in section 3.1.4.  40 

 ORAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
© 2020 O-RAN Alliance  All Rights Reserved 
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ  
102 
 
Note, an O-RU8 which supports IPv6 may infer that a VLAN is not used to support management plane communications 1 
if it receives an IPv6 Router Advertisement without either the “managed address configurat ion” or “other 2 
configuration” bits set. 3 
An O-RU8 may have been previously con figured with managem ent plane VLAN information, for example storing the 4 
last VLAN(s) used for management plane connectivity, and/or being previously configure d with a range of management 5 
plane VLANs by a NETCONF client that has been stored in reset -persistent memory. The O -RU8 may use this 6 
information to optimize its discovery of the VLAN ID(s) used for management plane connectivity.  7 
If the O -RU8 does not have previously con figured management plane VLAN information, the O -RU8 shall attempt to 8 
discover DHCP servers on all of its Ethernet ports using untagged Ethernet frames.  9 
If the O -RU8 does not receive a DHCP OFFER from a DHCP server using untagged frames, or previously con figured 10 
VLANs, the O-RU8 should attempt to contact a DHCP server using individual VLANs on all of its Et hernet ports. 11 
A3.3.3.4 O-RU8 Management Plane IP Address Assignment 12 
Automatic IP address assignment for the O-RU8 management plane can be achieved using different techniques: 13 
1. IPv4 configuration using DHCPv4, RFC2131 [10] enables DHCP servers to configure  IPv4 network address(es) on 14 
the O-RU8. An O-RU8 shall support the behavior specified in RFC 4361 [33], using s table DHCPv4 node identifiers in 15 
their dhcp-client-identifier option. 16 
Note: a network realized with multiple DHCP servers should ensure that their config urations are co-ordinated to ensure 17 
a common default gateway is provisioned in an O -RU8 which receives multiple DHCPv4 responses, e.g., when received 18 
over different interfaces. 19 
Note: an O -RU8 may indicate that it supports configuration of routing informati on using RFC 3442, enabling static 20 
routes to be used by the O -RU8 when determining how to route uplink packets, e.g., when the O -RU8 supports multiple 21 
interfaces. 22 
For O-RU8s that support IPv6, both stateful and stateless address assignment procedures are s upported: 23 
2. IPv6 Stateless Address Auto -Configuration (SLAAC), RFC4862 [11] enables the O -RU8 to generate link -local and 24 
global addresses. 25 
Note: a network realized with multiple IPv6-enabled routers that support dynamic address assignment is expected to u se 26 
RFC 4191 to configure the preference of the default route prefixes learnt by the O-RU8 using SLAAC. 27 
3. IPv6 State -full address configuration uses  DHCPv6, RFC3315 [12] and enables DHCP servers to con figure IPv6 28 
network address(es) on the O-RU8. DHCPv6 is transported using UDP, using the link- local address on the O-RU8 and a 29 
link-scoped multicast address on the DHCP server.  30 
Note: the above does not restrict the realization of the DHCP server, which may be integrated with the O -DU8, may be 31 
provided by the transport system, or may be accessed via a relay. 32 
The DHCP server should operate using static bindings, i.e., ensuring an O -RU8 identified by a particular client hardware 33 
address will be re-allocated the same management plane IP address, e.g., after perfor ming an O-RU8 reset procedure. 34 
A3.3.3.5 O-RU8 Controller Discovery 35 
This section provides how to automatically discover the O-RU8 Controller address(es). 36 
O-RU8s that have obtained their IPv6 addresses by stateless address auto -configuration, shall use stateless DHCP v6, 37 
RFC3736 [13], to obtain NETCONF management plane configuration information.  38 
Other O -RU8s operating using stateful IPv4 or IPv6 address allocations shall obtain NETCONF management plane 39 
configuration information during IP address allocation. 40 
The O-RU8 as NETCONF Server shall be able to recover NETCONF Client information using the following DHCP 41 
Options, draft-ietf-netconf-zerotouch [14]:  42 
• DHCPv4 OPTION V4_ZEROTOUCH_REDIRECT [143] 43 

 ORAN.WG7.IPC-HRD-Opt8.0-v01.00 
 
© 2020 O-RAN Alliance  All Rights Reserved 
Your use is subject to the terms of the O-RAN Adopter License Agreement in Annex ZZZ  
103 
 
• DHCPv6 OPTION_V6_ZEROTOUCH_REDIRECT [136] 1 
These options are defined in  [14] and are used to deliver bootstrap- server-list information to the O -RU8. The O -RU8 2 
shall use these options to recover the NETCONF client in formation using the above IANA defined DHCP options. The 3 
O-RU8 is not required to implement the remainder of the zerotouch capabilities defined in [14].  4 
The above DHCP option provided information is encoded as a list of one or more server URIs, of the for mat 5 
“https://<ip-address-or-hostname>[:<port>]” signaled to the O -RU8. The DHCP server shall ensure that all NET CONF 6 
client information is encoded with these options. 7 
Other O -RU8s which have had their IP address(es) manually con figured, shall also have the ir O -RU8 Controller(s) 8 
manually configured. 9 
For IPv4, the O -RU8 may request the OPTION_V4_ZEROTOUCH_REDIRECT by including its option code in the 10 
Parameter Request List (55) in DHCP discover/request messages. 11 
For IPv6, the O -RU8 may request the OPTION_V6_ZEROTOUCH_REDIRECT option by including the requested 12 
option codes in the Option Request Option. 13 
Note, these operations are optional because the DHCP server will already be aware that it is communicating with an O -14 
RU8 based on the recovered vendor class option. 15 
To enable O -RU8s to operate in legacy environments where the DHCP server has not been enhanced with IANA 16 
defined DHCP options for zero touch NETCONF capability, an O -RAN defined vendor specific option can be used to 17 
signal a ll NETCONF client information to the O -RU8 using option 43 for DHCPv4 and option 17 for DHCPv6. 18 
Multiple instances of NETCONF client information may be signaled, encoded as a sequence of type/length/value fields.  19 
The definition of the types used within the  DHCPv4 option 43/DHCPv6 Option 17 depends on the vendor -class option 20 
reported by the O-RU8 in its DHCP messages. 21 
When a legacy O-RU8 reports its vendor-class using the “o-ran-ru” prefix, the following types are defined: 22 
Type: 0x01 – O-RU8 Controller IP Address 23 
Type: 0x02 – O-RU8 Controller Fully Qualified Domain Name 24 
When the O-RU8 reports its vendor-class using the “o-ran-ru2” prefix, the following types are defined: 25 
Type: 0x81 – O-RU8 Controller IP Address 26 
Type 0x82 – O-RU8 Controller Fully Qualified Domain Name 27 
In all cases, the Type is followed by the length, which is the hexadecimal encoding of length of value field in octets, and 28 
the Value. 29 
When Type corresponds to an O-RU8 Controller IP Address, the value encodes IPv4 address(es) in hexadecimal format. 30 
For example, a single server with IPv4 address 198.185.159.144 will be encoded in an option 43 TLV as  31 
Type 0x81 (or x01 for legacy) 32 
Length: 0x04  33 
Value: C6 B9 9F 90 34 
When Type corresponds to an O -RU8 Controller Fully Qualified Domain Name, this encodes the string representation 35 
of domain name, using ACSII encoding (i.e., following for encoding used for the domain name in the Host Name 36 
DHCP Option 12). For example, a server with FQDN “controller.operator.com” will be encoded in an option 43 TLV as  37 
Type 0x82 (or x02 for legacy)  38 
Length: 0x17  39 
Value: 63 6F 6E 74 72 6F 6C 6C 65 72 2E 6F 70 65 72 61 74 6F 72 2E 63 6F 6D  40

The format of the DHCPv6 opt ion 17 follows the format of the DHCPv4 encoding, with the additional inclusion of an 1 
Enterprise Number prior to the TLV option data. The IANA allocated private enterprise number to be used with 2 
DHCPv6 option 17 is 53148. 3 
A3.3.3.6  Multi-Vendor Plug-and-Play 4 
As described in sub -section 3.3.2, an O -RU8 may optionally support certificate enrollment using CMPv2. 3GPP 32.509 5 
specifies how the O-RU8 can discover the IP address or FQDN of one or more Certification Authority (CA/RA) servers 6 
using DHCP Option 43. 7 
In additio n to the DHCPv4 encoding using Option 43, an O -RU8 supporting IPv6 and certificate enrollment using 8 
CMPv2 shall additionally support the signaling of vendor specific options using DHCPv6 option 17. The format of the 9 
DHCPv6 option 17 follows the format of t he DHCPv4 encoding, with the additional inclusion of an Enterprise Number 10 
prior to the TLV option data. The IANA allocated private enterprise number to be used with DHCPv6 option 17 is 11 
53148 (as allocated by IANA to O-RAN Alliance). 12 
An O-RU8 shall report any discovered multi-vendor plug-and-play servers using the o-ran-dhcp YANG model. 13 
A3.3.4 NETCONF Call Home to O-RU8 Controller(s) 14 
The O-RU8 aims to have NETCONF sessions with all of the known O -RU8 Controller(s), either discovered usin g the 15 
DHCP options defined i n Section 3.1.4, provisioned by an existing NETCONF client, or statically con figured. An O -16 
RU8 controller may attempt to autonomously initiate a NETCONF session with the O -RU8, e.g., triggered by some out 17 
of band interaction with the IP address management functionality. In order to support NETCONF clients corresponding 18 
to known O -RU8 Controllers that either do not attempt to initiate a NETCONF session with the O -RU8, or are 19 
prohibited from doing so, e.g., because of Network Address Translation limitations, the O -RU8 shall call home to all 20 
known O-RU8 Controllers with which it does not already have an active NETCONF session.  21 
If the O-RU8 is unable to establish a NETCONF session with any of the O -RU8 Controller(s), the O -RU8 shall use the 22 
“re-call-home-no-ssh-timer” to repeatedly re- perform the call home procedure to all with which is does not have an 23 
established NETCONF session.  24 
The O -RU8 shall use RFC 8071 [15] whereby the O -RU8 (NETCONF Server) initiates a TCP connection to the 25 
NETCONF client. The port used  by the O -RU8 shall be the one signaled using the draft -ietf-netconf-zerotouch DHCP 26 
option, else, if no port was signaled, the O -RU8 shall use the IANA -assigned port 4334. When the NETCONF client 27 
accepts a TCP connection on the allocated port, it initiates  an SSH session with the NETCONF Server. Using this SSH 28 
session, the NETCONF client initiates a NETCONF session. 29
